T 1532199478 24*	eremitah has quit (Ping timeout: 276 seconds)
T 1532199481 18<doug16k18>	the page tables are basically an array, where the address space is split into 1024 ranges of 1024 4KB pages
T 1532199496 18<bluezinc18>	doug16k: ok, so it's implemented in hardware?
T 1532199502 18<doug16k18>	yes
T 1532199511 18<bluezinc18>	that makes a heck of a lot more sense.
T 1532199607 18<doug16k18>	but it's a 2 level lookup. first it looks up the 4MB region containing the address being accessed, then it finds out where that page table is from the page directory. then, it reads the page table pointed to by that page directory entry, and looks up the page table entry for the 4KB region, and gets the physical address of that virtual address
T 1532199633 18<geist18>	right. it's functionally one large array, split into two levels so that you dont have to allocate it all at once
T 1532199718 18<bluezinc18>	I was trying to figure out how on earth it would work without doing something like that.  And I assume that the page tables are normally cached by the CPU to speed things up, right?
T 1532199733 18<doug16k18>	yes, the page table cache is called the TLB
T 1532199740 18<doug16k18>	Translation Lookaside Buffer
T 1532199789 18<doug16k18>	it's not quite as automatic as the data and instruction cache. you have to tell it to discard entries in some cases
T 1532199827 24*	SopaXorzTaker has quit (Remote host closed the connection)

T 1532199921 18<doug16k18>	basically whenever you reduce the permissions on an entry (like going from read/write to read only, or going from kernel page to user page, changing the physical address of a page, or making it not present at all because you swapped it to disk, or freed it) then you have to flush tlb entries (or the whole TLB in extreme cases)

T 1532299428 18<MrOnlineCoder18>	is it okay to use my pmm to allocate memory for paging structures like page tables or page directories instead of kernel heap?
T 1532299465 18<geist18>	yes
T 1532299472 18<geist18>	in fact you should absolutely do that

T 1532299508 23*	Vulcan (~Vulcan@rc137-3-88-172-220-120.fbx.proxad.net23) has joined
T 1532299509 18<MrOnlineCoder18>	ok ty
T 1532299557 18<geist18>	yes, it'd be a waste because if it were a regular heap it'd have to be a) 4096 byte aligned and b) would have some little tracking structure before it
T 1532299575 18<geist18>	so you'd end up needing to functionally allocate 4100 or so bytes, and thus you need essentially 2 pages to allocate a page
T 1532299599 18<clever18>	and the page tables have to be aligned, so you may wind up with holes in the heap before and after the page
T 1532299605 18<geist18>	plus when allocating pages for the page tables you generally need to allocate from a very low level allocator like the pmm
T 1532299610 18<geist18>	so that you dont end up recursing
T 1532299618 18<clever18>	something designed to give entire pages will always be aligned and never have overhead from holes
T 1532299622 18<geist18>	yah

T 1568341907 22âœ½	22geist has been digging into ARM external debugging port all day
T 1568341916 <geist>	looks like it's fairly straightforward to use one core to debug another
T 1568341925 <zid>	that sounds dirty
T 1568341954 <geist>	i'd been putting off reading how this stuff works for years, but once you get to it its pretty clever
T 1568341973 <geist>	the most clever part is there is no direct access to inspect the internal state of the core, but you can halt it and then shove one instruction at a time into it
T 1568341997 <geist>	and there's an easy place to write a word of data back to the thing doing the debugging (another processor or JTAG)
T 1568342007 <zid>	inject a bunch of mov [0xDEADBEEF], reg?
T 1568342011 <geist>	so basically you can dump the state of the core by shoving a series of instructions that just copy the value out
T 1568342016 <geist>	basically
T 1568342065 <geist>	so given that you can do pretty much anything you can think of to it
T 1568342110 <geist>	there are also a few priviledge instructions you can run only while being debugged, like an instruction that lets you directly set the processor mode (EL0, EL1, etc)
T 1568342131 <geist>	so if you break into a processor in user mode (EL0) you can just directly bump it to supervisor (EL1) and start going to town
T 1568342172 <geist>	since all the control registers that JTAG can see is also MMIO mapped, you can use one processor to do this to another
T 1568342195 <klange>	so you can check yourself before you wreck yourself?
T 1568342236 <geist>	well you can check your other doppleganger self before it trashes the place yeah

T 1568355593 <scapegoat0>	What the fuck is the difference between bootsector and mbr?
T 1568355704 <Mutabah>	Well.. that's quite forceful language for an initial question
T 1568355725 <scapegoat0>	Please x100, sorry x100
T 1568355734 <scapegoat0>	Good enough?
T 1568355743 <Mutabah>	A little too suck-up-y :)
T 1568355752 <Mutabah>	To answer your question, the difference is mostly semantics...
T 1568355775 <scapegoat0>	Difference between any two thing is semantics
T 1568355787 <Mutabah>	A bootsector is the first sector of a volume (which might be the start of a physical disk, or of a logical volume)
T 1568355830 <scapegoat0>	MBR is always start of a physical disk right?
T 1568355840 <Mutabah>	MBR is the term given to the first sector of a legacy hard disk, that contains both a small bootloader (that loads a bootsector from a partition) AND contains the partition table
T 1568355841 <clever>	i remember something about the old dos days, where the stub in sector 0 of the drive, would look at the partition table, and load+execute sector 0 of the PARTITION that is flagged as the boot partition
T 1568355893 <clever>	with modern bootloaders such as grub, there is a stage1 binary in the MBR, which loads stage 1.5 from a pre-defined location, and just ignores the partition table entirely
T 1568355920 <scapegoat0>	Cool thanks
T 1568355955 <clever>	grub-install is responsible for reading the partition tables, putting 1.5 at the right offset, and then modifying stage1 to contain that offset, and puttint it into the MBR
T 1568355982 <bcos_>	scapegoat0: For partitioned disks; "MBR" (in 1st sector of disk) typically contains some kind of boot manager that decides what to boot, and "VBR" (in 1st sector of partition) is the start of the operating system's boot stuff
T 1568356038 <bcos_>	(note that, for this case, MBR has nothing to do with any OS at all, unless it was a "single OS only" thing the OS installed for end-user convenience)
T 1568356115 <bcos_>	(also, things like GRUB try to combine "boot manager in MBR" with "boot laoder for many different operating systems that work in very different ways"; which mostly means it's a horrendeous design disaster)
T 1568356137 <scapegoat0>	Shit, I'll never be able to write a goddamn bootloader. I have been trying for 2 years now.
T 1568356163 <bcos_>	(like, "seperation of concerns" is totally ignored, and you end up with things like updating one OS and breaking every other OS)
T 1568356172 <clever>	i think grub can also install stage1 into the VBR, but that requires the filesystem to have enough empty space in its headers to allow it, and then an MBR to chain-load the VBR
T 1568356237 <bcos_>	Yeah; in theory it'd be possible to use one copy of GRUB as "boot manager only" to chainload other seperate copies of GRUB (on per OS, in the OS's own partition/s); but in that case you end up with a massive amount of unused bloat everywhere
T 1568356276 <clever>	the 3 main phases of grub, are stage 1, 1.5, and 2
T 1568356292 <clever>	stage 1 is typically under 512 bytes, and can only load stage 1.5 from a fixed position
T 1568356316 <ybyourmom>	u need an electric stone to evolve from stage 1.5 to 2
T 1568356322 <clever>	stage 1.5 is about 50-70kb-ish, and contains the main kernel for grub, and the modules for whatever fs /boot is on
T 1568356344 <clever>	stage 2 is then held in a file on /boot/, and implements the core of the UI and functionality
T 1568356370 <scapegoat0>	what the fuck guys, calm down
T 1568356376 <clever>	grub-install is responsible for figuring out the modules, and assembling stage 1.5
